Citizenship

Art 5–11 · Citizenship Act 1955 · CAA 2019 · Ways to Acquire/Lose Citizenship

📰
Live CA — Chapter 7 · Citizenship
CAA Implementation · NRC · OCI Rights · Dual Citizenship Debates
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA.
📊 30-Year Exam Pattern — Chapter 7
1–2
Prelims Qs per year
40%
Modes of acquiring citizenship
35%
Rights available only to citizens (Art 15, 16, 19, 29, 30)
25%
Citizenship Amendment Act & NRC
⚡ Pattern: India = single citizenship (unlike USA). Rights only for citizens: Art 15 (no discrimination), Art 16 (public employment), Art 19 (six freedoms), Art 29-30 (cultural & educational rights). CAA 2019 = most contested current affairs item. Parliament has exclusive power on citizenship (Art 11).
CONSTITUTIONAL PROVISIONS (Art 5–11)
ArticleProvision
Art 5Citizenship at commencement — domicile in India + born in India OR parents born in India OR 5 years resident before commencement
Art 6Rights of citizenship of persons migrated from Pakistan (before 19 Jul 1948 = automatic; after = registration)
Art 7Rights of persons migrated to Pakistan but returned — can apply for citizenship
Art 8Rights of persons of Indian origin residing outside India — can register with Indian diplomatic mission
Art 9Voluntary acquisition of citizenship of foreign state = NOT an Indian citizen
Art 10Continuance of citizenship rights subject to Parliament's law
Art 11Parliament has power to regulate citizenship — Parliament = sole authority
5 WAYS TO ACQUIRE CITIZENSHIP (Citizenship Act 1955)
🧠 Mnemonic — "B·D·R·N·I"
Birth · Descent · Registration · Naturalisation · Incorporation of Territory
1. Birth — born in India (with conditions since 1987, 2003 amendments). 2. Descent — father/parent is Indian citizen. 3. Registration — certain categories (e.g., persons of Indian origin). 4. Naturalisation — 12 years residence (or distinguished service). 5. Incorporation of Territory — if new territory becomes part of India (e.g., Goa 1961, Sikkim 1975).
3 WAYS TO LOSE CITIZENSHIP
1. Renunciation
  • Voluntary renunciation of Indian citizenship
  • Minor children also cease to be Indian citizens
  • But they can reclaim at 18
2. Termination
  • Voluntarily acquiring citizenship of another country
  • Automatic — no formal declaration needed
3. Deprivation
  • Compulsory; by order of Central Government
  • Grounds: fraud, disloyalty, anti-national activities during war, residing in enemy country 7+ years etc.
PYQs — Chapter 7
📜1990–2015▼
PRELIMS 1994
Which of the following rights is available to an alien (non-citizen) in India? (a) Right to equality (b) Right to freedom of religion (c) Right against exploitation (d) All of above
✅ (d) All of above — Most FRs are available to ALL PERSONS (including aliens). Rights ONLY for citizens: Art 15 (non-discrimination), Art 16 (public employment), Art 19 (six freedoms), Art 29-30 (cultural/educational). This distinction is a perennial Prelims question.
PRELIMS 2000
Under which article of the Constitution does Parliament have the power to regulate citizenship?
✅ Article 11 — Parliament has exclusive power to make provisions for acquisition and termination of citizenship. Citizenship Act 1955 is made under Art 11. No state legislature can make laws on citizenship.
PRELIMS 2014
If a non-citizen commits an offence in India, he/she cannot claim protection under: (a) Art 20 (b) Art 21 (c) Art 22 (d) Art 19
✅ (d) Art 19 — Art 19 (six freedoms) is available only to CITIZENS. Art 20 (protection in conviction), Art 21 (life & personal liberty), Art 22 (protection against arrest) are available to ALL PERSONS including non-citizens.
📔2016–2025▼
PRELIMS 2019 (CAA context)
Consider: 1. Parliament can by law create a class of citizens without the right to vote. 2. The Citizenship Act 1955 can be amended by Parliament.
✅ Both CORRECT — (1) OCI (Overseas Citizen of India) holders do not have voting rights or right to hold constitutional offices. (2) Parliament has full power under Art 11 to amend Citizenship Act 1955 — CAA 2019 is an exercise of this power.
MAINS 2020
The Citizenship Amendment Act, 2019 is seen by many as being in violation of the Constitution. Examine.
✅ Key arguments: FOR violation — Art 14 (equality), Art 15 (non-discrimination on religion), Art 25 (freedom of religion). AGAINST violation — Parliament's sovereign power under Art 11 to define citizenship; political persecution criterion; India = homeland for persecuted minorities of neighbouring countries. SC is examining the challenge. Link to Art 14's reasonable classification doctrine.
MAINS 2022
Discuss the Citizenship Amendment Act 2019 and its implications for India's constitutional framework.
✅ CAA 2019: Fast-tracks citizenship for persecuted minorities (Hindu, Sikh, Buddhist, Jain, Parsi, Christian) from Pakistan, Bangladesh, Afghanistan — excluding Muslims. Rules notified March 2024. Challenges: Art 14 (classification must be reasonable), secularism as basic structure, NRC linkage fears.
📰 Current Affairs 2024–25 — Chapter 7
MARCH 2024 · CAA RULES NOTIFIED
CAA Rules 2024 — Implementation Begins 11 March 2024
Central government notified the Citizenship Amendment Act 2019 rules on 11 March 2024, ahead of general elections. The rules lay down the procedure for applying for citizenship under CAA — online portal only. Excludes tribal areas of Assam, Meghalaya, Mizoram, Tripura (Sixth Schedule areas) and states under Inner Line Permit. SC is yet to hear the constitutional challenge. Over 20 states had passed resolutions against CAA. Static link: Parliament's exclusive power on citizenship (Art 11). The distinction between citizens (Art 15, 16, 19, 29, 30) and non-citizens directly relevant.
→ Prelims 2025-26: CAA rules, excluded areas; Mains: CAA and Art 14, secularism as basic structure
2024-25 · OCI RIGHTS CONTROVERSY
OCI Card Restrictions Upheld — SC 2024
SC upheld government's power to restrict OCI (Overseas Citizens of India) cardholders from certain rights. OCI ≠ full citizenship — no voting rights, no right to hold constitutional offices, cannot own agricultural/plantation land. 2024 — OCI cards cancelled for certain holders. Static link: Ch 7 — India has single citizenship. No dual citizenship. OCI is a form of permanent residency NOT citizenship. Citizenship Act 1955 Section 7D (cancellation of OCI).
→ Prelims: OCI rights and restrictions; Mains: Single citizenship as feature of Indian Constitution
08

Fundamental Rights

Part III · Art 12–35 · 6 Rights · Writs · Judicial Enforcement · Limitations

📰
Live CA — Chapter 8 · Fundamental Rights
SC Judgments on Art 21 · Writ Petitions · New FRs · Restrictions on FRs
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA.
📊 30-Year Exam Pattern — Chapter 8
4–6
Prelims Qs per year (HIGHEST chapter)
40%
Art 19 — 6 freedoms + restrictions
30%
Art 21 — expanding scope + SC cases
30%
Writs, Art 32, citizens vs non-citizens
⚡ Most tested chapter in all of Polity! Art 19 freedoms (which ones, which restrictions) — asked 8+ times. Art 21 scope (right to education, health, livelihood, privacy — Puttaswamy 2017) — asked 10+ times. 5 writs (know each writ's purpose). Art 32 = "heart and soul" (Ambedkar). Art 20 + 21 cannot be suspended even during National Emergency.
6 FUNDAMENTAL RIGHTS — Quick Map
Art 14–18
Right to Equality
Art 14: equality before law + equal protection. Art 15: no discrimination (sex, religion, race, caste, place of birth). Art 16: equal opportunity in public employment. Art 17: abolition of untouchability. Art 18: abolition of titles (except military/academic).
Art 19–22
Right to Freedom PRE ⭐⭐⭐⭐
Art 19: 6 freedoms (speech, assembly, association, movement, residence, profession). Art 20: protection in conviction. Art 21: life & personal liberty — "procedure established by law" + Maneka Gandhi expansion. Art 21-A: Right to Education (86th Amend 2002). Art 22: protection against arrest.
Art 23–24
Right against Exploitation
Art 23: prohibition of traffic in human beings + forced labour (beggars). Art 24: prohibition of child labour in factories/hazardous employment (below 14 years).
Art 25–28
Right to Freedom of Religion PRE ⭐⭐
Art 25: freedom of conscience + right to profess, practice, propagate religion. Art 26: manage religious affairs. Art 27: no tax for promotion of religion. Art 28: no religious instruction in state-funded institutions.
Art 29–30
Cultural & Educational Rights
Art 29: right to conserve culture, language, script (CITIZENS only). Art 30: minorities' right to establish & administer educational institutions. No discrimination in state aid to minority institutions.
Art 32
Right to Constitutional Remedies PRE ⭐⭐⭐
Ambedkar: "Heart and Soul of the Constitution." 5 writs: habeas corpus, mandamus, prohibition, certiorari, quo warranto. Art 32 itself is a Fundamental Right — cannot be suspended except during National Emergency (Art 359).
ART 19 — 6 FREEDOMS & RESTRICTIONS (Most Tested)
FreedomRestriction Grounds
Art 19(1)(a) — Speech & ExpressionSecurity of India, sovereignty & integrity, friendly relations with foreign states, public order, decency, morality, contempt of court, defamation, incitement to offence
Art 19(1)(b) — Assembly (peacefully, without arms)Sovereignty & integrity, public order
Art 19(1)(c) — Association/UnionSovereignty & integrity, public order, morality
Art 19(1)(d) — Movement throughout IndiaInterests of general public, protection of scheduled tribes
Art 19(1)(e) — Residence/SettlementInterests of general public, protection of scheduled tribes
Art 19(1)(g) — Profession/Trade/BusinessPublic interest, professional qualifications prescribed by law, state monopoly on trade
⚠️
Art 19(1)(f) — Right to Property was the original 7th freedom. Removed by 44th Amendment 1978. Now a legal right under Art 300-A only. This trap is asked 4+ times in 30 years.
5 WRITS — Know Each One
WritMeaningPurposeAgainst
Habeas Corpus"You may have the body"Release a person from illegal detentionPublic + Private authorities
Mandamus"We command"Command a public authority to do its legal dutyPublic authorities only
Prohibition"To prohibit"Prevent lower court/tribunal from exceeding jurisdictionJudicial/quasi-judicial bodies only
Certiorari"To certify"Quash order of lower court acting beyond jurisdictionJudicial/quasi-judicial bodies only
Quo Warranto"By what authority"Challenge person's right to hold public officePublic offices only
🧠 Mnemonic — Writs
H·M·P·C·Q = "Have More Patience, Child, Quit"
Habeas Corpus · Mandamus · Prohibition · Certiorari · Quo Warranto. Note: SC can issue all 5 under Art 32. HC can issue all 5 under Art 226 + MORE writs (wider jurisdiction than SC for writs). HC can issue writs for non-fundamental right violations too — SC cannot.
PYQs — Chapter 8 (Selected High-Impact)
📜1985–2005▼
MAINS 1986
What is Habeas Corpus? What is its significance in Indian democracy?
✅ Habeas Corpus = "produce the body." Issued against illegal detention. Protects personal liberty (Art 21). Significance: safeguard against executive arbitrariness. ADM Jabalpur 1976 — SC wrongly held HC suspended during Emergency. 44th Amendment 1978 corrected: Art 20 & 21 cannot be suspended even during Emergency.
PRELIMS 1991
Which writ can be issued against a private individual?
✅ Habeas Corpus — is the only writ that can be issued against BOTH public AND private individuals (if someone is illegally detaining another person). Mandamus, Prohibition, Certiorari, Quo Warranto — only against public authorities/offices.
PRELIMS 1995
Right to move the Supreme Court for enforcement of Fundamental Rights is itself a Fundamental Right — True?
✅ TRUE — Art 32 is itself a Fundamental Right. Ambedkar called it the "heart and soul of the Constitution." It cannot be suspended except under Art 359 during National Emergency. Art 226 (HC writ jurisdiction) is wider — can be invoked even for non-FR violations.
PRELIMS 2004
The writ that can be used to prevent a lower court from exceeding its jurisdiction is: (a) Habeas Corpus (b) Mandamus (c) Prohibition (d) Certiorari
✅ (c) Prohibition — issued BEFORE the order is made (preventive). Certiorari is issued AFTER an order is made (remedial/quashing). Both are against judicial/quasi-judicial bodies. Classic confusion between Prohibition and Certiorari — asked 3+ times.
📘2006–2020▼
MAINS 2011
Discuss the evolution of Right to Life (Art 21) through Supreme Court judgments.
✅ Key cases: AK Gopalan 1950 (narrow — only procedure in Art 21 matters). Maneka Gandhi 1978 (expanded — procedure must be fair, just, reasonable = convergence with "due process"). Francis Coralie 1981 (basic necessities of life). Olga Tellis 1986 (right to livelihood). Unni Krishnan 1993 (right to education). Puttaswamy 2017 (right to privacy). Current: Art 21 is the most expansive FR through judicial interpretation.
PRELIMS 2017
Consider: 1. The Right to Information Act is not directly included in FRs. 2. Freedom of speech under Art 19 includes freedom of the press. 3. Art 20 is available to both citizens and non-citizens.
✅ All three CORRECT. (1) RTI is derived from Art 19(1)(a) — not direct FR. (2) Freedom of press = part of freedom of speech (no separate press freedom in Art 19). (3) Art 20 is available to ALL persons including foreigners. Important multi-statement question.
PRELIMS 2020
Which of the following are not suspended even during a National Emergency under Art 352? 1. Art 20 2. Art 21 3. Art 19 4. Art 32
✅ Art 20 and Art 21 — cannot be suspended even during National Emergency (44th Amendment 1978 protection). Art 19 CAN be suspended during National Emergency. Art 32 can be suspended under Art 359 during NE. This is a VERY high-frequency trap.
📔2021–2025▼
MAINS 2021
Analyze distinguishing features of Right to Equality in USA and India.
✅ USA: 14th Amendment + "due process" = substantive equality tested by courts. India: Art 14-18 + "procedure established by law" = procedural focus; but Art 15-16 allow positive discrimination (reservations); Art 17 abolishes untouchability — more prescriptive than USA. India's approach = both formal + substantive equality.
PRELIMS 2022
Which of the following FRs can be suspended during a Proclamation of Emergency (Art 352)? (a) Art 19 only (b) Art 20 and 21 only (c) Art 14, 15, 19, 20, 21, 22 (d) All FRs except Art 20 and 21
✅ (d) All FRs except Art 20 and 21 — Under Art 358, Art 19 stands suspended automatically during external emergency. Under Art 359, President can suspend enforcement of other FRs EXCEPT Art 20 and 21. These two are the "untouchable" FRs.
PRELIMS 2023
Which of the following is not a Fundamental Right? (a) Right to privacy (b) Right to vote (c) Right to move SC for enforcement of FRs (d) Right against untouchability
✅ (b) Right to vote — Right to vote is a CONSTITUTIONAL right (Art 326) but NOT a Fundamental Right. Right to privacy = FR (Art 21, Puttaswamy 2017). Right to move SC = FR (Art 32). Right against untouchability = FR (Art 17). Classic trap — right to vote vs FR.
MAINS 2024
The Right to Privacy is an intrinsic part of Right to Life and Personal Liberty under Art 21. Discuss the implications of the Puttaswamy judgment.
✅ Puttaswamy v. Union of India (2017) — 9-judge bench unanimous: Right to Privacy = FR under Art 21. Three elements: (1) Informational privacy, (2) Privacy of body/personal choices, (3) Privacy of identity. Implications: AADHAAR (Information Privacy), decriminalisation of homosexuality (Navtej Johar 2018), reproductive rights, etc. But not absolute — can be restricted for legitimate state aims.
📰 Current Affairs 2024–25 — Chapter 8
2024–25 · ART 21 EXPANSION CONTINUES
Waqf Amendment Act 2024 — Art 14, 25, 26 Challenged
Waqf (Amendment) Act 2024 challenged in SC on grounds it violates Art 25-26 (religious freedom of Muslims), Art 14 (equality), and secularism as basic structure. SC issued stay on certain provisions in Nov 2024. Case involves fundamental questions about state power to regulate religious endowments. Static link: Art 25 (freedom of religion), Art 26 (religious denomination's right to manage affairs), Art 14 (equal protection). Classic tension between state's regulatory power and religious autonomy.
→ Mains: "State's power to regulate religious institutions vs FRs" — likely 2026 question
2024 · ART 21 — RIGHT TO PRIVACY
Digital Personal Data Protection Act 2023 — Implementation 2024-25
DPDPA 2023 (Digital Personal Data Protection Act) partially implemented in 2024. Rules being drafted. Gives individuals right to know how their data is used, right to correction, right to erasure. Static link: Flows directly from Puttaswamy 2017 (Right to Privacy = FR under Art 21). This is India's first comprehensive data protection law. "Data Fiduciary" obligations = giving substance to the right to informational privacy.
→ Mains: "Right to Privacy in digital age" + DPDPA as legislative framework
2025 · ART 19 — FREE SPEECH
Supreme Court on Digital Arrest Fraud + Free Speech Restrictions Online
SC 2024-25 heard multiple cases on government's power to block internet content, flag "fake news" under IT Rules 2021 (challenged in SC). Also, SC took suo motu cognizance of "digital arrest" scam. Static link: Art 19(1)(a) — freedom of speech and expression. Art 19(2) — restrictions. IT Rules 2021 (allowing govt to declare content as fake news) challenged as violating Art 19(1)(a).
→ Prelims: Art 19 restrictions; Mains: FRs in digital age
09

Directive Principles of State Policy

Part IV · Art 36–51 · Socialistic · Gandhian · Liberal-Intellectual · FR vs DPSP

📰
Live CA — Chapter 9 · DPSP
Welfare State Schemes · DPSP Implementation · FR vs DPSP Balance
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA.
📊 30-Year Exam Pattern — Chapter 9
2–3
Prelims Qs per year
40%
Classifying DPSP into 3 categories
35%
FR vs DPSP conflict & resolution cases
25%
Specific articles (Art 39, 44, 47, 48A, 51)
⚡ Pattern: DPSP borrowed from Irish Constitution (Art 45 of Irish Constitution). Art 44 (Uniform Civil Code) — asked almost every cycle. FR vs DPSP conflict (Champakam Dorairajan 1951, Golaknath 1967, Kesavananda 1973, Minerva Mills 1980) — perennial Mains topic. Which DPSP is Gandhian? (Cottage industries, panchayats, cow protection). Non-justiciable but "fundamental in governance."
3 TYPES OF DPSP
Socialistic DPSPs
  • Art 38: Social order for welfare
  • Art 39: Equal pay, adequate livelihood, children's health
  • Art 39-A: Equal justice and free legal aid
  • Art 41: Right to work, education, public assistance
  • Art 42: Just & humane conditions of work, maternity relief
  • Art 43: Living wage for workers
  • Art 43-A: Workers' participation in management
  • Art 47: Raise nutrition, standard of living, public health
Gandhian DPSPs
  • Art 40: Organise Village Panchayats
  • Art 43: Cottage industries in rural areas
  • Art 43-B: Promotion of co-operative societies (97th Amend 2011)
  • Art 46: Promote educational/economic interests of SC, ST
  • Art 47: Prohibit intoxicating drinks and drugs
  • Art 48: Organise agriculture, animal husbandry; prohibit slaughter of cows, calves
Liberal-Intellectual DPSPs
  • Art 44: Uniform Civil Code ⭐
  • Art 45: Free and compulsory education for children (now Art 21-A FR after 86th Amend)
  • Art 48-A: Protection of environment, forests, wildlife
  • Art 49: Protect monuments, places of national importance
  • Art 50: Separate judiciary from executive
  • Art 51: Promote international peace, security, treaty obligations
FR vs DPSP — Evolution of Conflict (Mains Perennial)
CaseYearRuling
Champakam Dorairajan1951FR prevails over DPSP; 1st Amendment passed to add Art 31-A (save land reform laws)
Golaknath1967Parliament CANNOT amend FRs; DPSPs subordinate
Kesavananda Bharati1973Parliament CAN amend FRs but cannot destroy Basic Structure
42nd Amendment1976DPSP given primacy over FRs (Art 31-C extended)
Minerva Mills198042nd Amendment's Art 31-C struck down; FR + DPSP = BALANCE = basic structure. "Bedrock of the balance."
Waman Rao1981Harmony between FR and DPSP must be maintained
PYQs — Chapter 9
📜1986–2015▼
MAINS 1986
What are Directive Principles of State Policy? Why are they non-justiciable?
✅ DPSP = guidelines to the state for making laws/policies; borrowed from Irish Constitution. Non-justiciable because Constitution itself (Art 37) says: no court shall enforce them BUT they are "fundamental in governance." Reason for non-justiciability: economic and social goals require resources, cannot be enforced like legal rights. Force = political (public opinion). But courts have used them to interpret FRs expansively (Art 21).
PRELIMS 1995
The Directive Principles of State Policy are borrowed from which constitution? (a) USA (b) UK (c) Ireland (d) Canada
✅ (c) Ireland — which borrowed it from Spanish Constitution. Key distinction: FRs from USA, DPSP from Ireland, Fundamental Duties from USSR. This source question is asked every cycle.
PRELIMS 2013
Which of the following DPSP has been implemented by the 73rd Constitutional Amendment? (a) Art 40 (b) Art 43 (c) Art 46 (d) Art 48
✅ (a) Art 40 — "The State shall take steps to organise village panchayats." 73rd Amendment 1992 gave constitutional status to panchayats (Part IX) — this directly implements the Gandhian DPSP of Art 40.
MAINS 2015
What was held in Minerva Mills case regarding the relation between FRs and DPSPs?
✅ Minerva Mills 1980: "The Indian Constitution is founded on the bedrock of the balance between the Fundamental Rights and the Directive Principles." Balance = basic structure. 42nd Amendment's attempt to give DPSP primacy over FRs (by expanding Art 31-C) was struck down as violating basic structure. Parliament cannot make DPSPs superior to FRs.
📔2016–2025▼
PRELIMS 2020
Which part of the Constitution declares the ideal of Welfare State?
✅ Part IV — DPSP (not Preamble, not FRs). DPSP contains the welfare state mandate: living wage, equal pay, free legal aid, workers' participation in management etc. Preamble mentions "justice" but DPSP is the instrument. Classic trap.
MAINS 2022
Uniform Civil Code (Art 44) — examine its relevance and challenges.
✅ UCC = Art 44 DPSP — common civil law for all citizens regardless of religion. SC has repeatedly reminded Parliament to implement UCC (Shah Bano 1985, Sarla Mudgal 1995, Pannalal Bansilal 2018). Challenges: religious diversity, minority rights (Art 25-26, 29-30), federal complications. Uttarakhand became 1st state to enact UCC (2024). Relevance: gender justice, national integration, Art 44 as a "beacon."
PRELIMS 2024
Which DPSP was the basis for the 73rd Constitutional Amendment Act, 1992?
✅ Article 40 — "The State shall take steps to organise village panchayats." 73rd Amendment added Part IX and Schedule 11 to give constitutional status to panchayati raj institutions, directly implementing this Gandhian DPSP.
📰 Current Affairs 2024–25 — Chapter 9
2024 · ART 44 · UCC
Uttarakhand Uniform Civil Code 2024 — First State to Implement UCC
Uttarakhand became the first state to implement Uniform Civil Code (UCC) in 2024. The UCC covers marriage, divorce, inheritance, and adoption, making them uniform across religions for residents. Other BJP-governed states are also considering UCC. SC noted in several judgments that Art 44 is a "neglected directive." Static link: Art 44 DPSP — "The State shall endeavour to secure for the citizens a uniform civil code throughout the territory of India." This is a Gandhian DPSP. Uttarakhand's action is the first legislative implementation.
→ Mains 2025-26: "Uniform Civil Code — constitutional mandate and challenges" — very high probability
2024–25 · ART 48A · ENVIRONMENT
Supreme Court on Right to Climate-Free Environment under Art 21 + Art 48A
SC in MK Ranjitsinh v. Union of India (2024) recognised the right to be free from adverse effects of climate change as a Fundamental Right under Art 21 (life and liberty) and Art 14 (equality). Also invoked Art 48A DPSP (protection of environment). Static link: Art 48A = Liberal-Intellectual DPSP on environmental protection. SC using DPSP to expand Art 21 FR — a classic FR-DPSP synthesis that started with Maneka Gandhi 1978.
→ Mains: "Environmental rights in India — DPSP to FR journey"

Fundamental Duties

Part IV-A · Art 51-A · 11 Duties · Swaran Singh Committee · Non-Justiciable

📰
Live CA — Chapter 10 · Fundamental Duties
FD Implementation · Environmental Duties · National Integration Duties
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA.
📊 30-Year Exam Pattern — Chapter 10
1
Prelims Qs per year (occasional)
50%
How many FDs, which Amendment added
30%
Specific duties — environmental, national flag
20%
Verma Committee recommendations
⚡ Pattern: 11 FDs (not 10). 42nd Amendment 1976 added 10 duties; 86th Amendment 2002 added 11th duty (provide education to children 6-14 years). Borrowed from USSR. Swaran Singh Committee recommended FDs. Verma Committee (1999) suggested making some FDs enforceable. Non-justiciable — no legal sanction if not followed.
11 FUNDAMENTAL DUTIES (Art 51-A)
Duties Added by 42nd Amendment 1976
  • (a) Abide by Constitution, respect National Flag, National Anthem
  • (b) Cherish noble ideals of national struggle
  • (c) Uphold sovereignty, unity, integrity of India
  • (d) Defend the country; render national service
  • (e) Promote harmony and spirit of common brotherhood
  • (f) Preserve rich heritage of composite culture
  • (g) Protect natural environment, wildlife, forests, rivers
  • (h) Develop scientific temper, humanism, inquiry, reform
  • (i) Safeguard public property; abjure violence
  • (j) Strive for excellence in all spheres of individual and collective activity
11th Duty — 86th Amendment 2002
  • (k) If a parent or guardian, to provide opportunities for education to child between age 6–14 years
  • Corresponds to Art 21-A (Right to Education) added simultaneously
  • Borrowed from USSR Constitution's model of combining rights with duties
KEY FACTS ABOUT FDs
FactDetail
Original FDs10 — added by 42nd Amendment 1976 on recommendation of Swaran Singh Committee
Current FDs11 — 11th added by 86th Amendment 2002
SourceBorrowed from USSR (now Russia) Constitution
NatureNon-justiciable — no legal sanction for violation. However, Parliament CAN enact laws to enforce them.
Verma Committee 1999Recommended legal sanctions for violation of certain FDs; some FDs already have legal backing (e.g., Prevention of Insults to National Honour Act 1971)
FDs in original 1950 Constitution0 — FDs were NOT in original Constitution
PYQs — Chapter 10
📜1990–2025▼
PRELIMS 1996
Fundamental Duties were incorporated in the Constitution by which Amendment?
✅ 42nd Constitutional Amendment Act, 1976 — on recommendation of Swaran Singh Committee. 10 duties added to Part IV-A (Article 51-A). 11th duty added later by 86th Amendment 2002.
PRELIMS 2004
Which of the following is NOT a Fundamental Duty? (a) Protect environment (b) Pay taxes regularly (c) Develop scientific temper (d) Safeguard public property
✅ (b) Pay taxes regularly — This is NOT a Fundamental Duty. The 11 FDs are specifically listed in Art 51-A and paying taxes is not among them. Classic "what is NOT in FDs" question type.
PRELIMS 2012
The idea of including Fundamental Duties in the Constitution was borrowed from: (a) American Constitution (b) Irish Constitution (c) USSR Constitution (d) French Constitution
✅ (c) USSR Constitution — This is the standard answer for source of FDs. Compare: FRs from USA, DPSP from Ireland, FDs from USSR. This source question appears every few years.
PRELIMS 2022
How many Fundamental Duties are mentioned in the Constitution of India?
✅ 11 Fundamental Duties — 10 added by 42nd Amendment 1976 + 11th added by 86th Amendment 2002. Many candidates mistakenly answer 10. The 11th duty: provide opportunities for education to child between 6–14 years.
📰 Current Affairs 2024–25 — Chapter 10
2024 · FD (g) — ENVIRONMENT
SC Recognises Climate Rights under Art 21 — Reinforces FD (g)
MK Ranjitsinh v. Union of India (2024) — SC recognised right against adverse climate change effects. FD (g) — "to protect and improve the natural environment including forests, lakes, rivers and wild life, and to have compassion for living creatures" — cited by SC as a citizens' duty that complements the state's Art 48A DPSP obligation and Art 21 FR. Static link: FDs are non-justiciable per se but SC uses them to reinforce government obligations under FRs and DPSP.
→ Mains: "Relationship between FDs, DPSP and FRs — complementary framework"
2024 · CONSTITUTION DAY — FDs FOCUS
Constitution Day 2024 — National Campaign on Fundamental Duties
26 November 2024: Ministry of Law launched awareness campaign on FDs alongside Constitution Day celebrations. Theme: citizens' responsibilities in democracy. Preamble + FDs recitation drives in schools and govt offices. Static link: FDs were added precisely to remind citizens that rights come with responsibilities. Swaran Singh Committee's rationale: rights without duties = imbalance in democracy.
→ Prelims: 11 FDs, which amendment added them; Mains: FDs and constitutional democracy

Amendment of the Constitution

Art 368 · 3 Types of Amendment · 106 Amendments · Basic Structure Doctrine

📰
Live CA — Chapter 11 · Amendment
ONOE Bill (129th Amendment) · Waqf Challenge · Basic Structure Debates
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA.
📊 30-Year Exam Pattern — Chapter 11
2–3
Prelims Qs per year
45%
3 Types of Amendment procedure
35%
Key amendments (42nd, 44th, 73rd-74th, 86th etc.)
20%
Basic Structure Doctrine cases
⚡ Pattern: 3 types of amendment procedure (simple majority, special majority, special majority + state ratification) — asked 5+ times. Which provisions need state ratification? (federal provisions). 42nd Amendment = "Mini-Constitution." 44th Amendment = corrected Emergency abuses. Basic Structure (Kesavananda 1973) = limit on Parliament's amendment power. ONOE Bill 2024 = current affairs hook.
3 TYPES OF AMENDMENT PROCEDURE
TypeProcedureExamples
Type 1 — Simple MajorityMajority of members present & voting in each House (like ordinary law). NOT under Art 368 technically.Admission of new states (Art 2), creation of new states (Art 3), abolish/create Legislative Council of a state, citizenship, elections, 5th/6th Schedule
Type 2 — Special Majority2/3rd of members present & voting PLUS majority of total membership of each House. Under Art 368.Most provisions — FRs, DPSP, FDs, elections, SC/HC, emergency, constitutional bodies
Type 3 — Special Majority + State RatificationType 2 PLUS ratification by at least HALF of state legislatures. Under Art 368.Federal provisions: election of President, executive power of Union/States, SC & HC, distribution of legislative powers, 7th Schedule (3 lists), representation of states in Parliament, Art 368 itself
⭐
Key distinction for Prelims: Type 1 (simple majority) is NOT covered by Art 368 — so these amendments are NOT constitutional amendments in the formal sense. Type 2 and Type 3 are covered by Art 368. Trap: "All constitutional amendments require 2/3rd majority" — FALSE. Type 1 only needs simple majority.

PYQs — Chapter 11
📜1990–2015▼
PRELIMS 1990
The Constitution of India can be amended by Parliament by a special majority and by ratification of state legislatures for: (a) All provisions (b) Federal provisions only (c) FR provisions only (d) All except FRs
✅ (b) Federal provisions only — Type 3 amendment (special majority + state ratification) is required only for provisions affecting the federal structure: President's election, power of Union/States, SC/HC, 7th Schedule, representation in Parliament, Art 368 itself.
MAINS 2001
The 42nd Constitutional Amendment 1976 is known as the "Mini-Constitution." Explain.
✅ 42nd Amendment made the MOST changes to the Constitution — added Preamble words (Socialist, Secular, Integrity), added FDs (Art 51-A), changed DPSP-FR relationship, curtailed judicial review, extended Emergency provisions, added Art 31-C, made 10 changes to Art 368. Hence "Mini-Constitution" — but Minerva Mills 1980 struck down several provisions.
MAINS 2015
Examine the procedural and substantive limitations on Parliament's amending power under Art 368.
✅ Procedural: 3 types (simple majority, special majority, special majority + state ratification). Substantive: Basic Structure doctrine (Kesavananda Bharati 1973). Elements of basic structure: supremacy of Constitution, republican/democratic form, secular character, separation of powers, federal character, judicial review, FRs etc. Parliament cannot destroy these even through Art 368.
📔2016–2025▼
PRELIMS 2022
Consider: 1. Constitution defines its "basic structure" in terms of federalism, secularism, FRs and democracy. 2. Constitution provides for "judicial review" to safeguard citizens' liberties.
✅ Statement 1 WRONG — Basic Structure is NOT defined in Constitution. It is a JUDICIAL doctrine (Kesavananda Bharati 1973). Statement 2 CORRECT — Art 32 (SC) + Art 226 (HC) provide judicial review. Answer: Only 2. Most frequently tested trap: "Constitution defines basic structure" = FALSE.
MAINS 2025
Examine procedural and substantive limitations on Parliament's amending power. (250w)
✅ Procedural: Art 368 — 3 types. Substantive: Basic Structure (Kesavananda 1973). ONOE Bill 2024 — does Art 82A violate federalism (basic structure)? Balram Singh 2024 — Socialist, Secular = basic structure. 23rd Law Commission: ONOE does NOT violate basic structure. Conclusion: India's amendment power = constrained sovereignty.
📰 Current Affairs 2024–25 — Chapter 11
DEC 2024 · 129TH AMENDMENT BILL MAINS ⭐⭐⭐
ONOE Bill — Constitution (129th Amendment) Bill 2024, Referred to JPC
Introduced 17 Dec 2024 in Lok Sabha. Proposes new Art 82A — simultaneous elections to Lok Sabha + State Assemblies. Based on Kovind Committee (2023-24) recommendations. Referred to JPC. Bills amend Arts 82, 83, 172, 327. Historical note: simultaneous elections existed 1951-1967. Critics: violates federal structure (basic structure). Law Commission: does NOT violate basic structure. Static link: Art 368 Type 2 amendment (special majority needed). Raises basic structure questions — does fixing electoral terms violate parliamentary democracy as basic structure?
→ Mains 2026: "ONOE and parliamentary democracy/federalism" — prepare both sides
2024-25 · BASIC STRUCTURE CONTINUOUSLY INVOKED
Multiple Constitutional Challenges in SC — All Invoke Basic Structure
ONOE Bill, Waqf Amendment Act 2024, CAA 2019 (pending), EWS Reservation (103rd Amendment upheld 2022 — 3-2), delimitation controversy — ALL involve Basic Structure arguments. In Janhit Abhiyan v. Union of India (2022), 3-2 SC majority upheld 103rd Amendment (EWS reservation). Dissent: violated equality as basic structure. Static link: Basic Structure doctrine is the "supreme law above Art 368" — Kesavananda 1973. Parliament's constituent power = unlimited EXCEPT for basic structure.
→ Prelims: Key basic structure elements; Mains: "Role of SC as guardian of basic structure"
12

Basic Structure of the Constitution

Kesavananda Bharati 1973 · Elements · Evolution · Application 2024-25

📰
Live CA — Chapter 12 · Basic Structure
ONOE + Basic Structure · Waqf + Secularism · Balram Singh 2024 · NJAC Aftermath
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA.
📊 30-Year Exam Pattern — Chapter 12
2–3
Prelims Qs per year + Mains every year
40%
Elements of Basic Structure
40%
Key cases — Kesavananda, NJAC, Minerva Mills
20%
"Basic Structure NOT defined in Constitution" trap
⚡ Pattern: "Constitution defines basic structure" = FALSE (judicial doctrine, not in Constitution text) — asked 4+ times. Elements of basic structure (supremacy of Constitution, secular character, federal structure, democratic republic, judicial review, separation of powers, FRs) — classifying these is a perennial Prelims and Mains topic. NJAC (struck down 2015 for violating judicial independence as basic structure) is the most recent major application of the doctrine.
EVOLUTION OF BASIC STRUCTURE DOCTRINE
1951 — Shankari Prasad v. UOI
Parliament CAN amend FRs
1st Amendment upheld. Parliament's amending power includes power to abridge/take away FRs.
1967 — Golaknath v. State of Punjab
Parliament CANNOT amend FRs
SC (11-judge bench, 6-5) reversed Shankari Prasad. FRs are "transcendental" — Parliament cannot amend them. Created crisis.
1971 — 24th Amendment
Parliament responded — Art 368 amended
Parliament added that Art 368 includes power to amend ANY provision of Constitution including FRs.
1973 — Kesavananda Bharati v. State of Kerala ⭐
Basic Structure Doctrine BORN (13-judge bench, 7-6)
Parliament CAN amend FRs (overruled Golaknath). BUT cannot alter the "BASIC STRUCTURE" of the Constitution. First time doctrine articulated. 24th Amendment upheld but with the basic structure limit.
1976 — 42nd Amendment
Parliament tried to override Basic Structure
Art 368(4) & (5) added: "No amendment shall be called in question in any court." Tried to make Parliament's constituent power absolute.
1980 — Minerva Mills v. UOI
42nd Amendment's override provisions STRUCK DOWN
SC struck down 42nd Amendment's clauses making Parliament's amendment power unlimited. Judicial review = basic structure. Balance between FR + DPSP = basic structure.
2015 — SC Advocates-on-Record v. UOI (NJAC)
99th Amendment + NJAC Act struck down
NJAC (National Judicial Appointments Commission) would have given executive role in judicial appointments. SC struck down as violating "independence of judiciary" = basic structure.
2024 — Balram Singh v. UOI
Secularism + Socialism = Basic Structure reaffirmed
SC dismissed challenge to "Socialist" and "Secular" in Preamble. Held these are integral to Basic Structure. Constitution is a living document. Kesavananda 1973 principles reaffirmed.
ELEMENTS OF BASIC STRUCTURE (Judicially Recognised)
ElementCase Where Recognised
Supremacy of the ConstitutionKesavananda Bharati 1973
Secular character of the ConstitutionKesavananda 1973; SR Bommai 1994; Balram Singh 2024
Democratic and republican form of governmentKesavananda 1973
Federal character of the ConstitutionKesavananda 1973; SR Bommai 1994
Separation of powersKesavananda 1973
Judicial ReviewMinerva Mills 1980
Balance between FR and DPSPMinerva Mills 1980
Free and fair electionsIndira Nehru Gandhi 1975
Independence of JudiciarySC Advocates-on-Record (NJAC) 2015
Rule of LawKesavananda 1973
Powers of SC under Art 32Kesavananda 1973
Unity and integrity of the nationKesavananda 1973
Parliamentary system of governmentKesavananda 1973
Sovereignty of IndiaKesavananda 1973
⚠️
Critical Prelims Trap (asked 2022, 2023): The Constitution of India does NOT define or list the "basic structure" anywhere in its text. The basic structure doctrine is a JUDICIAL CREATION by the Supreme Court in Kesavananda Bharati 1973. If a Prelims statement says "Constitution defines basic structure" — it is FALSE.
PYQs — Chapter 12
📜1988–2015▼
MAINS 1988
What is the "basic structure" doctrine? How was it developed?
✅ Doctrine = Parliament cannot amend Constitution to alter its basic structure. Development: Shankari Prasad 1951 (Parliament CAN amend FRs) → Golaknath 1967 (Parliament CANNOT amend FRs) → 24th Amendment 1971 (Parliament reasserted power) → Kesavananda 1973 (Parliament CAN amend but NOT basic structure). Classic Mains question asked in 1988, repeated in 2005, 2015, 2025 in different forms.
MAINS 2005
Examine the role of the Supreme Court in protecting the "Basic Structure" of the Indian Constitution.
✅ SC as guardian: Kesavananda 1973 (created doctrine), Minerva Mills 1980 (judicial review itself = basic structure), Indira Gandhi 1975 (free elections), NJAC 2015 (judicial independence). Present application: ONOE Bill 2024 (does it violate parliamentary democracy as basic structure?), Waqf Amendment 2024 (does it violate secularism?). SC = ultimate interpreter; Parliament = supreme law-maker WITHIN basic structure constraints.
📔2016–2025▼
PRELIMS 2022
Constitution defines basic structure in terms of federalism, secularism, FRs — True/False?
✅ FALSE — Constitution does NOT define basic structure. It is a judicial doctrine. Asked this exact way 3 times in recent years.
MAINS 2023
The 103rd Constitutional Amendment Act 2019 has been upheld by the Supreme Court. What are the key aspects of this decision?
✅ Janhit Abhiyan v. UOI (2022): 5-judge bench, 3-2 majority upheld 10% EWS reservation (103rd Amendment). Majority: EWS as a new classification does NOT violate equality as basic structure. Dissent (Justices Ravindra Bhat + Narasimha): violates equality = basic structure since it excludes SC/ST/OBC from EWS benefits. Shows basic structure doctrine is still contested — what constitutes equality is debated.
MAINS 2025
The Indian Constitution has conferred amending power on Parliament. Examine procedural and substantive limitations. (250w)
✅ Procedural (Art 368): 3 types. Substantive: Basic Structure doctrine. Key elements: federalism, secularism, democracy, judicial review, separation of powers. ONOE Bill 2024 = latest test: 23rd Law Commission says NO violation. Critics: violates parliamentary democracy (collective responsibility requires dissolution possibility). Balram Singh 2024: socialism, secularism reaffirmed as basic structure. India's amendment power = constrained sovereignty, NOT unlimited constituent power.
📰 Current Affairs 2024–25 — Chapter 12
2024 · BALRAM SINGH · MOST IMPORTANT
Balram Singh v. Union of India (2024) — Basic Structure Doctrine's Latest Application
SC (Constitution Bench) dismissed challenge to "Socialist" and "Secular" in Preamble. Key: Both terms are integral to Basic Structure. Constitution is a living document — the 1949 adoption date does not freeze its content. Secularism was implicitly present since inception. Socialism = welfare state commitment (not Marxist). Political controversy: ruling alliance members' calls to remove these words definitively rejected by SC. This is the most important 2024 case for UPSC 2026.
→ Directly answers: Mains 2024 (India as secular state), Mains 2016 (adjectives in Preamble). Must cite in any answer on Basic Structure or Preamble.
DEC 2024 · ONOE = BASIC STRUCTURE TEST
129th Amendment Bill — Parliament vs Basic Structure
ONOE (One Nation One Election) Bill proposes Art 82A — synchronise Lok Sabha and state assembly elections. Critics invoke Basic Structure: (1) Federalism — state assembly terms curtailed without consent (SR Bommai 1994 — states have independent constitutional identity). (2) Parliamentary democracy — collective responsibility requires ability to dissolve assembly. (3) Free elections — synchronising might distort state-level mandates. 23rd Law Commission: ONOE does NOT violate basic structure. SC will likely be the final arbiter. Static link: This bill tests 3 elements of basic structure simultaneously.
→ Mains 2026: ONOE and Basic Structure — prepare YES/NO both sides
2025 · KURIAN JOSEPH COMMITTEE
Tamil Nadu Committee Recommends Codifying Basic Structure in Art 368
High-Level Committee recommended inserting explicit "Basic Features" clause into Art 368 — listing federalism, democracy, secularism, judicial review, free elections as formally unamendable. This would transform the judicially-created doctrine into a textual constitutional provision. Arguments: prevents future erosion, increases certainty. Counter: reduces constitutional flexibility; SC already protects basic structure adequately. Static link: Basic Structure currently = judge-made law. This proposal would make it explicit text — bridging the gap between constitutional law and constitutional text.
→ Mains: "Should India codify the Basic Structure doctrine?" — new angle question

Parliamentary System

Westminster Model · vs Presidential · Features · PM Govt

📰
Live CA — Chapter 13 · Parliamentary System
Westminster Model · vs Presidential · Features · PM Govt
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA linked to this chapter.
KEY CONTENT FROM BOOK
CHAPTER 13 Parliamentary System The Constitution of India provides for a parliamentary form of government, both at the Centre and in the states. Articles 74 and 75 deal with the parliamentary system at the Centre and Articles 163 and 164 in the states. Modern democratic governments are classified into parliamentary and presidential on the basis of the nature of relations between the executive and the legislative organs of the government. The parliamentary system of government is the one in which the executive is responsible to the legislature for its policies and acts. The presidential system of government, on the other hand, is one in which the executive is not responsible to the legislature for its policies and acts, and is constitutionally independent of the legislature with respect of its term of office. The parliamentary government is also known as cabinet government or responsible government or Westminster model of government and is prevalent in Britain, Japan, Canada and India among others. The presidential government, on the other hand, is also known as non-responsible or non-parliamentary or fixed executive system of government and is prevalent in USA, Brazil, Russia and Sri Lanka among others. Ivor Jennings called the parliamentary system as ‘cabinet system’ because the cabinet is the nucleus of power in a parliamentary system. The parliamentary government is also known as ‘responsible government’ as the cabinet (the real executive) is accountable to the Parliament and stays in office so long as it enjoys the latter’s confidence. It is described as ‘Westminster model of government’ after the location of the British Parliament, where the parliamentary system originated. In the past, the British constitutional and political experts described the Prime Minister as ‘primus inter pares’ (first among equals) in relation to the cabinet. In the recent period, the Prime Minister’s power, influence and position have increased significantly vis-a-vis the cabinet. He/
PYQs — Chapter 13
📋 Previous Year Questions — Chapter 13 ▼
MAINS 1986
What is parliamentary form of government? Compare with Presidential form.
✅ Parliamentary: collective responsibility, PM as real executive, fusion of legislature + executive. Presidential (USA): separation of powers, fixed term, directly elected President. India chose parliamentary due to colonial Westminster experience, accountability needs, multi-party diversity.
PRELIMS 1999
Which of the following is NOT a feature of parliamentary government? (a) Collective responsibility (b) Judicial supremacy (c) PM leadership (d) Nominal executive
✅ (b) Judicial supremacy — associated with Presidential system (USA). Parliamentary features: collective responsibility, nominal + real executive, PM leadership, ministerial membership of legislature.
PRELIMS 2003
Which one of the following is NOT a feature of the parliamentary form? (b) Judicial supremacy
✅ Judicial supremacy — Parliamentary system is based on principle of parliamentary sovereignty (UK model) + cooperation between legislature & executive. Judicial supremacy characterises Presidential systems.
MAINS 2013
What are the essential features of parliamentary system of government? How does it differ from presidential system?
✅ Parliamentary: nominal + real executives, collective responsibility, PM is real executive, ministers are members of legislature, dissolution of lower house, majority party rule. Presidential (USA): direct election of President, separation of powers, fixed term, no collective responsibility. India = parliamentary but NOT sovereign Parliament (unlike UK).
MAINS 2023 (GS-II)
Compare and contrast British and Indian approaches to Parliamentary Sovereignty.
✅ UK: Parliament is ABSOLUTELY sovereign — no court can strike down Parliament's law. India: Parliament CONDITIONALLY sovereign — SC can review laws, Basic Structure limits Parliament, written Constitution is supreme. India = synthesis of UK parliamentary sovereignty + USA judicial supremacy.

Federal System

Federal vs Unitary · Quasi-Federal · India's Features

📰
Live CA — Chapter 14 · Federal System
Federal vs Unitary · Quasi-Federal · India's Features
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA linked to this chapter.
KEY CONTENT FROM BOOK
CHAPTER 14 Federal System Political scientists have classified governments into unitary and federal on the basis of the nature of relations between the national government and the regional governments. By definition, a unitary government is one in which all the powers are vested in the national government and the regional governments, if at all exist, derive their authority from the national government. A federal government, on the other hand, is one in which powers are divided between the national government and the regional governments by the Constitution itself and both operate in their respective jurisdictions independently. Britain, France, Japan, China, Italy, Norway, Sweden and so on have a unitary model of government while the US, Switzerland, Australia, Canada, Russia, Brazil, Argentina and so on have a federal model of government. In a federal model, the national government is known as the Federal government or the Central government or the Union government and the regional government is known as the state government or the provincial government. The specific features of the federal and unitary governments are mentioned in a comparative manner in Table 14.1. The term ‘federation’ is drived from a Latin word foedus which means ‘treaty’ or ‘agreement’. Thus, a federation is a new state (political system) which is formed through a treaty or an agreement between the various units. The units of a federation are known by various names like states (as in US) or cantons (as in Switzerland) or provinces (as in Canada) or republics (as in Russia). A federation can be formed in two ways, that is, by way of integration or by way of disintegration. In the first case, a number of militarily weak or economically backward states (independent) come together to form a big and a strong union, as for example, the US. In the second case, a big unitary state is converted into a federation by granting autonomy to the provinces to promote regional interest (for example, Canada).
PYQs — Chapter 14
📋 Previous Year Questions — Chapter 14 ▼
MAINS 1988
Discuss the federal features of the Indian Constitution.
✅ Federal features: 2 governments, 3-tier division of powers (7th Schedule), written constitution, supremacy of constitution, independent judiciary, bicameralism. BUT India also has strong unitary features. Always acknowledge quasi-federal nature. Cite K.C. Wheare.
PRELIMS 1997
Which feature of the Indian Constitution shows its federal character? (a) Independent Judiciary (b) FRs (c) Single citizenship (d) DPSP
✅ (a) Independent Judiciary — acts as arbiter between Centre and States. Single citizenship = UNITARY feature. FRs and DPSPs apply to all = not specifically federal.
MAINS 1995 + 2002 + 2008 + 2014 + 2022
The Constitution of India is described as 'Quasi-Federal' / Federal with unitary bias. Discuss.
✅ Asked 8+ times in 30 years — the single most repeated Mains polity question! Federal features (5 points) + Unitary features (7 points: Governor, Art 3, emergency provisions, all-India services, single citizenship) + Scholars (Wheare: quasi-federal, Austin: co-operative, Morris Jones: bargaining, Jennings: centralising tendency).
PRELIMS 2017
Consider: 1. India follows 'dual citizenship' like USA. 2. The word 'federal' does not appear in the Constitution.
✅ Statement 1 WRONG — India has SINGLE citizenship (unlike USA). Statement 2 CORRECT — Art 1 says 'Union of States' not 'Federation'. Both show unitary character.
MAINS 2022 (GS-II)
Though the federal principle is dominant in our Constitution, federalism leans in favour of a strong Centre. Discuss.
✅ EXACT REPEAT of 2014 question. Model: Federal features (3 lists, independent SC, bicameralism) + Unitary features (single citizenship, strong Centre, emergency converts federal→unitary, Governor appointment, all-India services, Art 3). Conclusion: purposive federalism = right balance for India's unity in diversity.

Centre–State Relations

Legislative · Administrative · Financial · Sarkaria Commission

📰
Live CA — Chapter 15 · Centre–State Relations
Legislative · Administrative · Financial · Sarkaria Commission
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA linked to this chapter.
KEY CONTENT FROM BOOK
CHAPTER 15 Centre–State Relations The Constitution of India, being federal in structure, divides all powers (legislative, executive and financial) between the Centre and the states. However, there is no division of judicial power as the Constitution has established an integrated judicial system to enforce both the Central laws as well as state laws. Though the Centre and the states are supreme in their respective fields, the maximum harmony and coordination between them is essential for the effective operation of the federal system. Hence, the Constitution contains elaborate provisions to regulate the various dimensions of the relations between the Centre and the states. The Centre-state relations can be studied under three heads: • Legislative relations. • Administrative relations. • Financial relations. LEGISLATIVE RELATIONS Articles 245 to 255 in Part XI of the Constitution deal with the legislative relations between the Centre and the states. Besides these, there are some other articles dealing with the same subject. Like any other Federal Constitution, the Indian Constitution also divides the legislative powers between the Centre and the states with respect to both the territory and the subjects of legislation. Further, the Constitution provides for the parliamentary legislation in the state field under five extraordinary situations as well as the centre’s control over state legislation in certain cases. Thus, there are four aspects in the Centre–states legislative relations, viz., • Territorial extent of Central and state legislation; • Distribution of legislative subjects; • Parliamentary legislation in the state field; and • Centre’s control over state legislation. 1. Territorial Extent of Central and State Legislation The Constitution defines the territorial limits of the legislative powers vested in the Centre and the states in the following way: 221 INDIAN POLIY BY M LAXMIKANTH 8TH EDITION (i) The Parliament can make laws for the whole

📋 Previous Year Questions — Chapter 15 ▼
MAINS 2000
Discuss the issues of federalism in India in the context of Centre-State relations.
✅ Issues: Governor appointment, Art 356 misuse (pre-SR Bommai), single party dominance, emergency provisions, all-India services, Finance Commission design. Solutions: Sarkaria Commission 1983, SR Bommai 1994. Classic Mains question asked EVERY year in some form.
PRELIMS 2013
Consider: 1. Under Art 254, if State law repugnant to Central law in Concurrent List, Central law prevails. 2. Central law takes precedence if both State and Centre occupy same field.
✅ Both CORRECT — Art 254 doctrine of repugnancy. But if State law has received Presidential assent, it prevails over earlier Central law (Art 254(2)). Critical for Centre-State legislative relations.
MAINS 2016
Critically examine the role of Governor in Centre-State relations.
✅ Governor = Centre's agent in state. Issues: appointment by Centre (partisan), discretionary powers, giving/withholding assent to bills (Art 200), imposition of President's Rule (Art 356). SR Bommai 1994 restricted Art 356 misuse. Punchi Commission 2007 recommended fixed term. Governor should be 'constitutional head' not political tool.
MAINS 2020
Centralizing tendencies of Indian federalism — are they consistent with the federal spirit?
✅ Centre's powers: Art 3 (unilateral state boundary change), Art 356 (President's Rule), all-India services, Governor appointment, Concurrent List expansion, Finance Commission design. Federal spirit: spirit of co-operation, consultation, fiscal autonomy. Sarkaria, Punchhi, Kurian Joseph (2025) committees recommended rebalancing.
MAINS 2024 (GS-II)
Suggest measures to build trust between Centre and States and strengthen federalism.
✅ Sarkaria Commission: Governor should be eminent non-partisan person; Art 356 = last resort; residual powers to states. Punchhi Commission: Governor cannot indefinitely withhold assent; reform Finance Commission. Kurian Joseph Committee 2025: amend Art 368 for state ratification; move Education to State List; revise GST Council voting.

Inter-State Relations

Art 262–263 · Water Disputes · Interstate Council · Zonal Councils

Live CA — Chapter 16 · Inter-State Relations
Art 262–263 · Water Disputes · Interstate Council · Zonal Councils
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA linked to this chapter.
KEY CONTENT FROM BOOK
CHAPTER 16 Inter-State Relations The successful functioning of the Indian federal system depends not only on the harmonious relations and close cooperation between the Centre and the states but also between the states inter se. Hence, the Constitution makes the following provisions with regard to inter-state comity: 1. Adjudication of inter-state water disputes. 2. Coordination through inter-state councils. 3. Mutual recognition of public acts, records and judicial proceedings. 4. Freedom of inter-state trade, commerce and intercourse. In addition, the zonal councils have been established by the Parliament to promote inter-state cooperation and coordination.1 INTER-STATE WATER DISPUTES Article 262 of the Constitution provides for the adjudication of inter-state water disputes. It makes two provisions: (i) Parliament may by law provide for the adjudication of any dispute or complaint with respect to the use, distribution and control of waters of any inter-state river and river valley. (ii) Parliament may also provide that neither the Supreme Court nor any other court is to exercise jurisdiction in respect of any such dispute or complaint. Under this provision, the Parliament has enacted two laws [the River Boards Act (1956) and the InterState River Water Disputes Act (1956)]. The River Boards Act provides for the establishment of river boards for the regulation and development of inter-state river and river valleys. A river board is established by the Central government on the request of the state governments concerned to advise them. The Inter-State River Water Disputes Act empowers the Central government to set up an ad hoc tribunal for the adjudication of a dispute between two or more states in relation to the waters of an inter-state river or river valley. The decision of the tribunal would be final and binding on the parties to the dispute. Neither the Supreme Court nor any other court is to have jurisdiction in respect of any water dispute which may be referred

Which article deals with adjudication of disputes relating to waters of inter-state rivers?

Emergency Provisions

Art 352 · Art 356 · Art 360 · Effects on Centre-State

📰
Live CA — Chapter 17 · Emergency Provisions
Art 352 · Art 356 · Art 360 · Effects on Centre-State
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA linked to this chapter.
KEY CONTENT FROM BOOK
CHAPTER 17 Emergency Provisions The Emergency provisions are contained in Part XVIII of the Constitution, from Articles 352 to 360. These provisions enable the Central government to meet any abnormal situation effectively. The rationality behind the incorporation of these provisions in the Constitution is to safeguard the sovereignty, unity, integrity and security of the country, the democratic political system, and the Constitution. During an Emergency, the Central government becomes all powerful and the states go into the total control of the Centre. It converts the federal structure into a unitary one without a formal amendment of the Constitution. This kind of transformation of the political system from federal during normal times to unitary during Emergency is a unique feature of the Indian Constitution. In this context, Dr. B.R. Ambedkar observed in the Constituent Assembly that1: ‘All federal systems including American are placed in a tight mould of federalism. No matter what the circumstances, it cannot change its form and shape. It can never be unitary. On the other hand, the Constitution of India can be both unitary as well as federal according to the requirements of time and circumstances. In normal times, it is framed to work as a federal system. But in times of Emergency, it is so designed as to make it work as though it was a unitary system.’ The Constitution stipulates three types of emergencies: 1. An emergency due to war, external aggression or armed rebellion2 (Article 352). This is popularly known as ‘National Emergency’. However, the Constitution employs the expression ‘proclamation of emergency’ to denote an emergency of this type. 2. An Emergency due to the failure of the constitutional machinery in the states (Article 356). This is popularly known as ‘President’s Rule’. It is also known by two other names—‘State Emergency’ or ‘constitutional Emergency’. However, the Constitution does not use the word ‘emergency’ for this situation. 3. Financia
PYQs — Chapter 17
📋 Previous Year Questions — Chapter 17 ▼
MAINS 2001
What are the different kinds of emergencies under the Indian Constitution? Describe each.
✅ 3 types: (1) Art 352 — National Emergency (war/external aggression/armed rebellion — NOTE: 'internal disturbance' changed to 'armed rebellion' by 44th Amend 1978). (2) Art 356/365 — State Emergency/President's Rule. (3) Art 360 — Financial Emergency (never proclaimed). Effects: National → FRs (except 20, 21) suspended.
PRELIMS 2014
'Internal disturbance' as a ground for proclamation of National Emergency has been replaced by which term?
✅ 'Armed rebellion' — 44th Constitutional Amendment Act, 1978. Raised the bar significantly for proclaiming National Emergency. 'Internal disturbance' was seen as too vague and had been misused.
PRELIMS 2018
Consider: 1. Proclamation of National Emergency requires approval of both Houses of Parliament within one month. 2. During National Emergency, the Parliament can legislate on subjects in State List.
✅ Both CORRECT — (1) Special majority (2/3rd + majority of total membership) within 1 month. (2) During National Emergency, Parliament's legislative power extends to State List subjects (Art 250). State governments continue to function but Parliament can override.
MAINS 2019
Examine the impact of National Emergency on federal structure of India.
✅ Federal→Unitary transformation without formal amendment. Parliament can legislate on State List. Executive power of Union extends to give directions to states. Art 83 — Lok Sabha term can be extended one year at a time. Art 19 stands suspended (external emergency). Art 20, 21 cannot be suspended. SR Bommai 1994 restricted Art 356.
PRELIMS 2022
Which of the following emergency has NEVER been proclaimed in India? (a) National Emergency (b) State Emergency (c) Financial Emergency (d) All have been proclaimed
✅ (c) Financial Emergency (Art 360) — has NEVER been proclaimed in India. National Emergency: proclaimed 1962, 1971, 1975. State Emergency (President's Rule): proclaimed many times. Financial Emergency: 0 times.

President

Election · Powers · Art 74 · Ordinance · Veto · Immunity

📰
Live CA — Chapter 18 · President
Election · Powers · Art 74 · Ordinance · Veto · Immunity
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA linked to this chapter.
KEY CONTENT FROM BOOK
CHAPTER 18 President Articles 52 to 78 in Part V of the Constitution deal with the Union executive. The Union executive consists of the President, the Vice-President, the Prime Minister, the council of ministers and the attorney general of India. The President is the head of the Indian State. He/she is the first citizen of India and acts as the symbol of unity, integrity and solidarity of the nation. ELECTION OF THE PRESIDENT The President is elected not directly by the people but by members of electoral college consisting of: 1. the elected members of both the Houses of Parliament; 2. the elected members of the legislative assemblies of the states; and 3. the elected members of the legislative assemblies of the Union Territories of Delhi and Puducherry1. Thus, the nominated members of both the Houses of Parliament, the nominated members of the state legislative assemblies, the members (both elected and nominated) of the state legislative councils (in case of the bicameral legislature) and the nominated members of the Legislative Assembly of Puducherry do not participate in the election of the President. The Legislative Assembly of Delhi has no nominated members. The 104th Constitutional Amendment Act of 2019 has not extended further the nomination of AngloIndian members to the Lok Sabha and State Legislative Assemblies. In other words, the amendment discontinued the provision of special representation of the Anglo-Indian community in the Lok Sabha and State Legislative Assemblies by nomination. Consequently, this provision ceased to have effect on the 25th January 2020. Further, where an assembly is dissolved, the members cease to be qualified to vote in the presidential election, even if fresh elections to the dissolved assembly are not held before the presidential election. The Constitution provides that there shall be uniformity in the scale of representation of different states as well as parity between the states as a whole and the Union at the election of the
PYQs — Chapter 18
📋 Previous Year Questions — Chapter 18 ▼
PRELIMS 1993
The President of India is elected by: (a) Lok Sabha only (b) Rajya Sabha only (c) Both Houses of Parliament (d) Elected members of Parliament + elected members of State Legislative Assemblies
✅ (d) — Art 54: Electoral College consists of elected members of Parliament (Lok Sabha + Rajya Sabha) AND elected members of State Legislative Assemblies (not MLCs). Elected members of Union Territories with legislature (Delhi + Puducherry) also vote.
PRELIMS 2000
The President of India exercises the power to promulgate ordinances under which article?
✅ Article 123 — President can promulgate ordinance when Parliament is not in session. Ordinance has same force as Act of Parliament. Must be laid before Parliament when it reassembles; ceases 6 weeks after reassembly or earlier if disapproved.
MAINS 2003
Discuss the constitutional position of the President of India.
✅ Nominal executive (de jure) — real power with Cabinet. 44th Amendment: President MUST act on Cabinet's advice. But can send back for reconsideration once (Art 74(1) proviso). Pocket veto possible (no time limit to assent to bills other than Money Bill). Immense discretionary power in hung Parliament situations.
PRELIMS 2014
Consider: 1. President can withhold assent to an ordinary Bill. 2. President can return an ordinary Bill to Parliament for reconsideration.
✅ Both CORRECT — (1) President can pocket veto (no time limit). (2) President can return once for reconsideration — but if Parliament passes it again (with or without amendment), President MUST give assent. Cannot return Constitutional Amendment Bills.
MAINS 2018
How are the powers of the President of India different from those of the President of the USA?
✅ India: nominal/constitutional head — PM and Cabinet exercise real power; Art 74 binding advice; parliamentary system. USA: real executive — separation of powers, directly elected, fixed 4-year term, no collective responsibility, genuine veto power, Commander-in-Chief with real authority.
PRELIMS 2022
The Presidential election in India involves the concept of 'value of votes'. The value of an MLA's vote depends on: (a) State's population (b) Number of elected MLAs (c) Both (d) Neither
✅ (c) Both — Value of MLA's vote = State population ÷ (Elected MLAs × 1000). States with larger population and fewer MLAs give each MLA a higher vote value. This ensures parity between Parliament and state assemblies.

Vice-President

Election · Powers · Chairman of Rajya Sabha · Art 63–71
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA linked to this chapter.
KEY CONTENT FROM BOOK
CHAPTER 19 Vice-President The Vice-President occupies the second highest office in the country. He/she is accorded a rank next to the President in the official warrant of precedence. This office is modelled on the lines of the American Vice-President. ELECTION The Vice-President, like the President, is elected not directly by the people but by the method of indirect election. He/she is elected by the members of an electoral college consisting of the members of both Houses of Parliament.1 Thus, this electoral college is different from the electoral college for the election of the President in the following two respects: 1. It consists of both elected and nominated members of the Parliament (in the case of president, only elected members). 2. It does not include the members of the state legislative assemblies (in the case of President, the elected members of the state legislative assemblies are included). Explaining the reason for this difference, Dr. B.R. Ambedkar observed:2 “The President is the head of the State and his/her power extends both to the administration by the Centre as well as to the states. Consequently, it is necessary that in his/her election, not only members of Parliament should play their part, but the members of the state legislatures should have a voice. But, when we come to the Vice-President, his/her normal functions are to preside over the council of states. It is only on a rare occasion, and that too for a temporary period, that he/she may be called upon to assume the duties of the president. That being so, it does not seem necessary that the members of the state legislatures should also be invited to take part in the election of the Vice-President”. But, the manner of election is same in both the cases. Thus, the Vice-President’s election, like that of the President’s election, is held in accordance with the system of proportional representation by means of the single transferable vote and the voting is by secret ballot. All doubts and dis

PRELIMS 1996
The Vice-President of India is elected by: (a) Lok Sabha only (b) Rajya Sabha only (c) Members of both Houses of Parliament (d) Elected members of both Houses
✅ (c) Members of BOTH Houses of Parliament — ALL members (elected + nominated) of Lok Sabha AND Rajya Sabha. Unlike President (elected members only + state assemblies). This distinction is frequently tested.
PRELIMS 2001
The Vice-President of India holds office for a term of: (a) 5 years (b) 6 years (c) 4 years (d) During the pleasure of the President
✅ (a) 5 years — Can be re-elected (no limit on number of terms). Can be removed by a resolution of Rajya Sabha passed by majority + agreed to by Lok Sabha. Note: unlike President, VP can be removed by simple majority.
MAINS 2005
Discuss the role of the Vice-President in the Indian constitutional scheme.
✅ Two roles: (1) Ex-officio Chairman of Rajya Sabha — presides over Rajya Sabha, casting vote in tie, maintains order. (2) Acts as President during vacancy or inability. VP does NOT have any independent executive powers unlike USA VP.
PRELIMS 2017
Who among the following acts as the President of India when both the President and Vice-President are unavailable?
✅ Chief Justice of India — Under Art 65, VP acts as President. If VP also unavailable, CJI discharges presidential functions (under Art 65 read with Art 126).

Prime Minister

Appointment · Powers · Art 74–75 · PM vs President · Cabinet
Click Update Now to fetch this week's CA linked to this chapter.
KEY CONTENT FROM BOOK
CHAPTER 20 Prime Minister In the scheme of parliamentary system of government provided by the constitution, the President is the nominal executive authority (de jure executive) and Prime Minister is the real executive authority (de facto executive). In other words, President is the head of the State while Prime Minister is the head of the government. APPOINTMENT OF THE PRIME MINISTER The Constitution does not contain any specific procedure for the selection and appointment of the Prime Minister. Article 75 says only that the Prime Minister shall be appointed by the President. However, this does not imply that the President is free to appoint any one as the Prime Minister. In accordance with the conventions of the parliamentary system of government, the President has to appoint the leader of the majority party in the Lok Sabha as the Prime Minister. But, when no party has a clear majority in the Lok Sabha, then the President may exercise his/her personal discretion in the selection and appointment of the Prime Minister. In such a situation, the President usually appoints the leader of the largest party or coalition in the Lok Sabha as the Prime Minister and asks him/her to seek a vote of confidence in the House within a month. This discretion was exercised by the President, for the first time in 1979, when Neelam Sanjiva Reddy (the then President) appointed Charan Singh (the coalition leader) as the Prime Minister after the fall of the Janata Party government headed by Morarji Desai. There is also one more situation when the President may have to exercise his/her individual judgement in the selection and appointment of the Prime Minister, that is, when the Prime Minister in office dies suddenly and there is no obvious successor. This is what happened when Indira Gandhi was assassinated in 1984. The then President Zail Singh appointed Rajiv Gandhi as the Prime Minister by ignoring the precedent of appointing a caretaker Prime Minister.1 Later on, the Congress parliame
PYQs — Chapter 20
📋 Previous Year Questions — Chapter 20 ▼
MAINS 1990
Discuss the powers and position of the Prime Minister in the Indian political system.
✅ PM = real executive. Powers: heads Cabinet, allocates portfolios, coordinates government policy, liaison between President and Cabinet (Art 78), chairs Cabinet meetings. 'Primus inter pares' (first among equals) in theory but in practice, PM dominates. Rajya Sabha membership possible.
PRELIMS 2005
Which article deals with the appointment of the Prime Minister?
✅ Article 75 — 'The Prime Minister shall be appointed by the President and the other Ministers shall be appointed by the President on the advice of the Prime Minister.' Also: PM must be a member of Parliament (either House). CoM collectively responsible to Lok Sabha (Art 75(3)).
PRELIMS 2014
The Prime Minister communicates to the President all decisions of Cabinet under which article?
✅ Article 78 — Duties of PM: (a) communicate to President all decisions of Council of Ministers; (b) furnish such information relating to administration as President may call for; (c) if President so requires, to submit for consideration of CoM any matter decided by a Minister.
MAINS 2013
The PM of India has emerged as the most powerful functionary in the governmental structure. Do you agree?
✅ YES — PM dominates: appoints/dismisses ministers, controls Cabinet agenda, single communication channel to President (Art 78), controls Parliament through majority, leads foreign policy. BUT: dependent on parliamentary majority; coalition era weakened PM; President has role in hung Parliament. 'Prime Ministerial Government' rather than 'Cabinet Government'.

CHAPTER 21 Central Council of Ministers As the Constitution of India provides for a parliamentary system of government modelled on the British pattern, the council of ministers headed by the prime minister is the real executive authority is our politico-administrative system. The principles of parliamentary system of government are not detailed in the Constitution, but two Articles (74 and 75) deal with them in a broad, sketchy and general manner. Article 74 deals with the status of the council of ministers while Article 75 deals with the appointment, tenure, responsibility, qualification, oath and salaries and allowances of the ministers. CONSTITUTIONAL PROVISIONS Article 74—Council of Ministers to aid and advise President 1. There shall be a Council of Ministers with the Prime Minister at the head to aid and advise the President who shall, in the exercise of his/her functions, act in accordance with such advice. However, the President may require the Council of Ministers to reconsider such advice and the President shall act in accordance with the advice tendered after such reconsideration. 2. The advice tendered by Ministers to the President shall not be inquired into in any court. Article 75—Other Provisions as to Ministers 1. The Prime Minister shall be appointed by the President and the other Ministers shall be appointed by the President on the advice of the Prime Minister. 2. The total number of ministers, including the Prime Minister, in the Council of Ministers shall not exceed 15% of the total strength of the Lok Sabha. This provision was added by the 91st Amendment Act of 2003. 3. A member of either house of Parliament belonging to any political party who is disqualified on the ground of defection shall also be disqualified to be appointed as a minister. This provision was also added by the 91st Amendment Act of 2003. 4. The ministers shall hold office during the pleasure of the President. 5. The council of ministers shall be collectively responsible to th

PRELIMS 2001
The principle of collective responsibility of Council of Ministers to Lok Sabha is laid down in which article?
✅ Article 75(3) — 'The Council of Ministers shall be collectively responsible to the House of the People.' This means Cabinet must resign if it loses confidence of Lok Sabha.
PRELIMS 2012
Which Constitutional Amendment Act fixed the size of the Council of Ministers?
✅ 91st Constitutional Amendment Act, 2003 — Fixed total strength of CoM including PM at not more than 15% of the total strength of the House of the People (Lok Sabha). Minimum 12 members. Same for states.
MAINS 2016
Distinguish between the Cabinet and the Council of Ministers.
✅ Council of Ministers = all ministers (Cabinet Ministers + Ministers of State + Deputy Ministers). Cabinet = inner circle of senior ministers (usually 15-20) who collectively decide government policy. Cabinet is subset of CoM. Only Cabinet ministers attend Cabinet meetings. PM may not include all senior ministers in Cabinet.
PRELIMS 2020
The 'Kitchen Cabinet' refers to: (a) Cabinet Committees (b) Informal group of PM's trusted advisors (c) CoM meeting kitchen affairs (d) Sub-group of Cabinet
✅ (b) Informal group of PM's trusted advisors — Informal inner cabinet. PM's personal advisors, trusted colleagues. Not a constitutional or statutory body. Examples: Nehru's inner circle, Indira Gandhi's kitchen cabinet.
